ESSAYS,

MORAL,     ECONOMICAL,     AND     POLITICAL.
BY    FRANCIS    BACON,

BAKON    CF    VERULaM,    VISCOUNT    ST.    ALBAN's,    AND    LORD-
HIGH-CHANCELLOR    OF    ENGLAND.

CONDUCT    OF    THE    UNDERSTANDING.

Y  JOHN  LOCKE,  ESQ.

AN   INTRODUCTOIIY   ESSAY,
BY  A.  POTTER,  D.  D.

PROFESSOR   OF   MORAL   PHILOSOFHY   IN    DNION   COLLKOB.

NEW-YORK:

HARPER  AND  BROTHERS,  C  L  I  F  F- S  TREE  T.

1844.

Entered,  according  to  Act  of  Congress,  in  the  year  1841,  by

Hakpek  &  Brothers,

In  the  Clerk's  Office  of  the  Southern  District  of  New-York,

The  best  known  and  most  popular  of  all  Bacon's  works
is  the  small  volume  to  which  he  has  given  the  title  of  Es-
says. It  is  also  one  of  those  where  the  superiority  of  his
genius  appears  to  the  greatest  advantage  ;  the  novelty  and
depth  of  his  reflections  often  receiving  a  strong  relief  from
the  triteness  of  his  subject.  It  may  be  read  from  begin-
ning to  end  in  a  few  hours ;  and  yet,  after  the  twentieth
perusal,  one  seldom  fails  to  remark  in  it  something  over-
looked before.

DuGALD  Stewart.

BACON   AND   LOCKE.

These  are  proud  names  in  the  history  of  Eng-
lish philosophy.  There  can  be  little  doubt  that
for  the  last  two  centuries  their  writings  have  done
more  to  determine  the  course  of  philosophical  and
scientific  inquiry,  than  any  or  all  others  in  our  Ian-
guage.  Before  one  of  them  even  national  jealousy
has  given  way,  and  Bacon  is  now  installed  by  uui-
versal  consent  as  the  Legislator  of  Science.  If
Locke  has  failed  to  gain  the  same  wide  and  undispu-
ted  sway,  it  must  be  attributed  as  much  to  the  time
at  which  he  wrote,  and  the  nature  of  the  subjects
to  which  he  applied  himself,  as  to  the  inferiority  of
his  genius.  His  name  is  inseparably  associated
with  the  restoration  to  the  world  of  sounder  meth-
ods  of  philosophising  ;  and  any  errors  or  fallacies
which  may  taint  his  own  speculations  are  more
than  counterbalanced,  a  thousand  times,  by  the
healthy  and  manly  tone  with  which  he  has  imbued
prevailing  habits  of  thought.

To  cultivate  an  acquaintance  with  these  great
masters  must  be  an  object  of  cherished  interest,
not  merely  with  the  scholar,  but  with  all  who  would
A2

VI  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

improve  their  minds.  Among  their  works  are
some  not  only  free  from  the  language  of  the
schools  and  level  to  the  general  understanding,  but
which,  to  use  Bacon's  own  phrase,  "  come  home
to  men's  business  and  bosoms."  Of  this  kind  are
those  comprised  in  the  present  volume.  They  fur-
nish as  striking  and  characteristic  specimens  of
their  respective  authors  as  could  well  be  brought
within  the  same  compass,  and  are  fraught  with
precious  lessons  for  the  heart,  as  well  as  for  the
understanding.  It  is  not  easy  to  conceive  in  what
way  the  press,  in  its  secular  capacity,  can  confer
a  greater  benefit  on  the  world,  than  by  reproducing,
in  a  cheap  and  portable  form,  these  choice  re-
mains of  our  older  classics.  It  somewhat  resem-
bles, yet  greatly  surpasses,  that  benefit  which  the
naturalist  confers  when  he  spreads  before  us  his
collection  of  plants  gathered  on  some  distant  coast,
or  opens  to  us  a  cabinet  richly  stored  with  the  fos-
sil  remains  of  extinct  races.  Poor  indeed,  and
most  inadequate,  are  the  notions  which  we  gain
of  the  orders  represented  by  the  specimens  of  the
naturalist,  compared  with  those  which  we  gain  of
a  great  writer  when  we  peruse  his  works.  These
are  no  petrified  or  withered  memorials  of  what
has  been.  They  are  instinct  with  a  present  and
imperishable  life.  We  learn  the  secret  of  the
spell  which  these  teachers  and  prophets  have  cast

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  Vl»

over  the  human  mind,  for  we  feel  it  ourselves.
On  the  offspring  of  their  genius  they  have  left  an
image  of  themselves,  which  time  not  only  fails  to
obscure,  but  which  can  often  be  read,  at  the  dis-
tance even  of  centuries,  more  clearly  than  it  was
read  by  contemporaries.

A  brief  comparison  of  these  sages,  and  of  the
services  which  they  have  rendered  to  science  and
humanity,  may  serve  as  an  appropriate  introduction
to  the  following  pages.

Bacon  and  Locke  had  little  in  common  in  their
intellectual  organization.  Both  doubtless  loved
truth  with  a  sincere  and  fervid  love.  Both,  too,
though  constitutionally  timid,  had  great  mental  in-
dependence, and  both  reposed  with  confidence  on
conclusions  which  had  been  deliberately  reached.
But  Bacon  had  a  greater  compass  of  intellectual
view,  and  a  more  penetrating  sagacity — Locke
more  patience  and  fondness  for  detail.  Bacon  had
a  power,  in  which  he  has  never  been  surpassed,  of
contemplating  many  and  distant  objects  at  once,
and  without  indistinctness  or  confusion — Locke
had  more  of  that  calm  judgment  and  good  sense,
which  enable  us  to  balance  opposing  errors  and
probabilities,  and  to  counsel  wisely  in  the  ordi-
nary affairs  of  life.  The  one  had  a  boianess  which
made  him  prompt  to  cope  with  the  difficulties
of  any   subject,  while   it   never  allowed   him   to

Vlll  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

shrink  from  avowing  his  opinions,  nor  from  accept-
ing the  consequences  to  which  they  seemed  to  lead.
The  other  was  more  cautious ;  and,  as  he  reached
his  conclusions  more  slowly,  so  he  sometimes  held
them  with  a  doubtful  grasp,  and  as  if  he  feared  the
consequences  with  which  they  might  be  charged.
Bacon's  intellect  was  irradiated  by  a  fancy  at
once  most  brilliant  and  yet  most  subdued  ;  a  fancy
which — seeming  to  seize  with  intuitive  ease  the
remotest  analogies,  and  yet  kept  in  wholesome  sub-
jection to  the  judgment — served  as  a  torch  to  light
his  way  into  the  most  hidden  recesses.  Locke
was  not  destitute  of  the  same  faculty,  but  with
him  it  was  not  so  much  an  element  of  discovery  —
a  prophetic  sense,  by  which  he  was  enabled  to
anticipate  truths  not  yet  revealed,  as  a  power,
by  which  he  could  illustrate  and  recommend  to
others  the  convictions  of  his  own  mind.  The
classical  studies  of  one  commenced  while  Europe
was  yet  rejoicing  over  the  revival  of  ancient  learn,
ing,  and  when,  of  course,  those  studies  had  a  fresh-
ness and  a  charm  not  now  known.  The  other
came  into  life  when  England  was  agitated  with
political  and  religious  discussions  of  the  most  ab-
sorbing character,  and  when  the  state  of  the  times
combined  with  his  own  personal  taste  and  experi-
ence to  divert  him  from  antiquity.  Bacon  seemed
endowed  with  all  high  faculties,  being  eminent  at

BACON    AND   LOCKE.  IX

once  as  a  wit  and  a  man  of  business,  as  a  courtier
and  a  judge,  a  companion,  a  public  speaker,  and  a
philosopher.*  Locke,  on  the  other  hand,  seems
to  have  been  made  for  retirement,  and  to  have  dis-
played his  finest  gifts,  as  well  as  found  his  chief
pleasures,  in  the  intercourse  of  a  few  friends,  and
in  the  tranquil  labours  of  the  study.

If  from  their  intellectual  we  pass  to  their  social
and  moral  qualities,  we  shall  find  that,  with  some
points  of  resemblance,  there  is  still  a  prevailing
and  marked  contrast.  Both  were  born  with  noble
instincts  and  aspirations  ;  both  naturally  loved  jus-
tice ;  and,  though  Locke's  temper  was  somewhat
prone  to  severity,  the  hearts  of  both  seem  to  have
overflowed  with  generous  and  kindly  feelings.  A
keen  relish  for  society,  and  for  the  pleasures  of

*  "  The  queen,"  says  the  Earl  of  Essex,  in  a  letter  to  Bacon
himself,  "  did  acknowledge  you  had  a  great  wit,  and  an  excellent
gift  of  speech,  and  much  other  good  learning.  But  in  law  she
rather  thought  you  could  make  show,  to  the  utmost  of  your
knowledge,  than  that  you  were  deep."  Against  this  judgment  of
Queen  Elizabeth  may  be  recorded  the  fact  that  none  of  Bacon's
decrees  as  chancellor  were  ever  set  aside,  and  the  following
among  many  other  tributes  to  his  surpassing  powers  as  a  jurist.
After  passing  a  high  encomium  on  the  ability  displayed  by  Ba-
con in  his  "  Reading  on  the  Statute  of  Uses,"  Hargrave  adds,
"  What  might  we  not  have  expected  from  the  hands  of  such  a
master,  if  his  vast  mind  had  not  so  embraced  within  its  com-
pass the  whole  field  of  science  as  very  much  to  detach  him
from  professional  studies." — Stewart's  Prelim.  Diss.,  part  i.,
note  F.

X  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

conversation,  seems  also  to  have  characterized
both  these  extraordinary  men.  By  Locke,  talking
was  regarded  as  the  best  means  of  maturing  and
rectifying  our  opinions ;  and  the  admirable  powers
of  Shaftesbury  in  this  respect  are  supposed  to  ex-
plain our  philosopher's  devotion  to  the  person  and
fortunes  of  a  man  so  unprincipled.  The  charm
and  inexhaustible  variety  of  Bacon's  conversation
are  thus  commemorated  by  Osborn  :  "  My  memory
doth  not  direct  me  towards  an  example  more  splen-
did  in  this  kind  than  the  Lord  Bacon,  who  in  all
companies  did  appear  a  good  proficient,  if  not  a
master,  in  those  arts  entertained  for  the  subject  of
every  one's  discourse.  His  most  casual  talk  de-
serveth  to  be  written.  As  I  have  been  told,  his
first  copies  required  no  great  labour  to  render
them  competent  for  the  nicest  judgment.  I  have
heard  him  entertain  a  country  lord  in  the  proper
terms  relating  to  hawks  and  dogs,  and  another  time
outcant  a  London  chirurgeon.  Nor  did  an  easy
falling  into  arguments  appear  less  an  ornament  in
him.  The  ears  of  his  hearers  received  more  grat-
ification than  trouble,  and  were  no  less  sorry
when  he  came  to  conclude,  than  displeased  with
any  who  did  interrupt  him  :  all  which  rendered
him  no  less  necessary  than  admirable  at  the  coun-
cil table,  where,  in  reference  to  impositions,  monop-
olies,   &c.,    the    meanest    manufactures    were   a

EACON    AND    LOCKE.  '  XI

usual  argument ;  and  in  this  he  baffled  the  Earl  of
Middlesex,  who  was  born  and  bred  a  citizen. "

In  all  other  respects,  the  character  and  life  of
Bacon  present  a  melancholy  contrast  to  those  of
Locke.  Bacon  had  a  patron,  than  whom  none
was  ever  more  kind ;  bu#t  must  remain  on  rec-
ord, to  his  unending  disgrace,  that  when  that  pa-
tron needed,  if  not  his  active  aid,  at  least  his  for-
bearance, even  so  poor  a  boon  was  refused  ;  and  to
propitiate  the  smiles  of  royalty,  he  offered  his
great  powers,  uninvited,  to  consummate  the  ruin  of
this  earliest  and  most  constant  friend.  Bacon  was
successively  the  servant  of  two  princes,  both  ca-
pricious and  both  arbitrary ;  and  to  obtain  or  pre-
serve their  favour,  he  did  not  hesitate  to  stoop  to
the  most  fulsome  flattery,  and  to  professions  of
unconditional  subserviency,  while  he  dealt  in  the
meanest  insinuations  against  his  rivals.*  Between
himself  and  the  court  favours  for  which  he  longed
so  anxiously,  stood  the  profligate  Buckingham  ;
and  yet,  to  gain  the  support  of  a  minion  so  worth-
less, "venerable  Bacon,"  the  father  of  Experimental
Philosophy,  could  submit  to  be  made  the  instrument
of  a  shameless  and  almost  boundless  rapacity.
Bacon,  again,  held  the  highest  trust  in  the  gift  of
his  sovereign,  and  was  invested  with  the  most  sol-

*  See,  as  an  example,  the  letter  addressed  to  King  James,  so-
liciting the  appointment  of  lord-chancellor.

Xll  BACON    AND   LOCKE.

emn  judicial  functions  ;  and  yet,  under  his  own  sig-
nature, owned  by  himself  to  be  his  "  act,  his  hand,
his  heart,"  he  pleads  guilty  to  three-and-twenty  in-
stances of  bribery  and  official  corruption.  Much,
doubtless,  must  be  placed  to  the  account  of  the
corrupt  and  servile  age|pn  which  he  lived ;  much,
also,  to  the  weakness  of  a  mind  which,  though  in
some  respects  transcendent,  could  never  withstand
the  seductions  of  pomp,  nor  place  proper  restraint
on  the  cupidity  of  followers.  With  all  this  abate-
ment, however,  his  derelictions  still  remain  too  fla-
grant for  pardon.  Not  only  in  his  labours  as  a
courtier,  but  when  composing  works  for  posterity,
which  he  himself  "  conceived  might  last  as  long  as
books  last,"*  he  sometimes  condescends  to  teach
Kingcraft,  and  seems  to  forget  that  the  glory  of  a
prince  is  of  less  moment  than  the  happiness  of  his
people.  While  the  general  tone  of  his  philosophy
is  lofty,  it  must  still  be  confessed  that  he  occa-
sionally counsels  in  strains  which  belong  rather  to
the  worldling  than  the  sage  ;  that  his  lessons  for
private  as  well  as  public  life  are  sometimes  too
pliant  and  equivocal  for  those  who  would  follow
only  where  truth  and  honour  lead  the  way.f     Let

*  See  his  Dedication  of  the  Essays  to  the  Duke  of  Bucking
ham.

t  See  Essays  on  the  True  Greatness  of  Kingdoms— on  Em.
pire— on  Simulation  and  Dissimulation— on  Cunning,  &c.

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  XlU

not  history  draw  a  veil  over  such  "  infirmities  of
noble  minds."  Great  talents  are  a  great  trust.
They  "  to  whom  much  has  been  given"  should  be
held  by  the  world,  as  well  as  by  their  Creator,  to
the  strictest  accountabiUty ;  and  if  they  prove  un-
faithful, they  should  be  set  before  the  young  as  bea-
cons, to  warn  them  against  the  peril,  as  well  as
guilt,  of  having  great  parts  and  powers  not  conse-
crated by  virtue.

How  grateful  to  turn  from  such  a  character  to
the  undimmed  lustre  which  surrounds  the  name  of
Locke.  He  too  had  a  patron,  and  one,  as  we
have  intimated,  not  without  great  faults ;  but,  in-
stead of  deserting  him  in  his  hour  of  adversity,  he
clung  to  him  only  the  more  closely ;  and  the  con-
stancy with  which  he  did  so,  through  every  vicissi-
tude, has  occasioned  the  only  suspicion  ever  cast
upon  his  name.  He  was  successively  a  citizen  of
the  commonwealth,  and  a  subject  of  the  second
Charles,  the  second  James,  and  the  third  William.
Maintaining  his  own  principles  with  calmness  and
dignity,  he  kept  aloof,  in  each  case  alike,  from  the
part  of  the  servile  courtier  and  from  that  of  the
factious  malcontent.  No  office  found  him  seeking
its  emoluments  at  the  expense  of  self-respect,  will-
ing to  wait  and  solicit  after  repeated  rebuffs  and
indignities.  When  the  revolution  of  1688,  of  which
he  had  been  no  inactive  spectator,  was  achieved,
B

XIV  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

he  was  urged  to  accept  an  honourable  and  some-
what lucrative  mission  to  a  foreign  court.  He  de-
clined, consenting  to  occupy  an  humbler  station,
but  one  which  he  insisted  on  resigning  so  soon  as
his  age  and  infirmities  interfered  with  its  active
duties.  His  whole  life  evinced  an  ardent  desire  to
serve  his  country  and  his  race  with  a  free-will
service.  Though  his  writings  provoked  severe
criticism,  and  his  political  opinions  made  him  the
object  of  bitter  persecution,  no  whispers  injuri-
ous to  his  honour  have  gained  lasting  credit.  In
his  speculations  as  a  metaphysician  and  political
philosopher,  he  has  laid  himself  open  to  the  charge
of  preparing  the  way  for  licentious  principles  and
immoral  practice  ;  but  no  one  professes  to  believe
that  he  foresaw  such  consequences  himself,  or  that
he  would  not  have  deprecated  them  as  the  greatest
of  evils.  The  evening  of  his  days  was  spent  in
communing  with  the  Scriptures.  His  death  was
that  of  the  Christian.  His  practical  counsels  were
always  on  the  side  of  duty  and  truth  ;  and  while
it  must  be  said  of  Bacon  that  his  philosophy  was
better  than  his  life,  it  may  be  said  of  Locke,  as  of
few  others  who  have  written  for  the  instruction
and  admiration  of  the  world,  that  his  life  was  even
better  than  his  philosophy.

In  order  to  form  a  just  estimate  of  their  merits
as  philosophers,  it  is  important  to  view  them  in  the

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  XV

light  of  the  respective  eras  to  which  they  belonged.
The  direction  taken  by  a  great  mind  in  its  re-
searches and  speculations  depends  almost  entirely
on  the  state  of  Politics  and  of  Religion.  In  Ba-
con's time,  under  the  stern  sway  of  a  Tudor  and  a
Stuart,  it  would  have  been  fatal  to  a  public  man
to  have  raised,  or  even  entertained,  a  question  re-
specting the  limits  of  royal  prerogative,  either  in
church  or  state.  The  spirit  of  free  inquiry,  which
was  already  beginning  to  pervade  and  agitate  the
mass  of  the  nation,  kept  studiously  aloof  from  the
precincts  of  the  court ;  and  it  need  not  surprise
us,  therefore,  that  with  all  his  sagacity,  even  Ba-
con should  have  remained  through  life  so  uncon-
scious of  a  movement  which  was  about  to  convulse
all  England  to  its  centre,  and  which  was  destin-
ed to  call  up  for  unrestrained  scrutiny  every  prin-
ciple  of  government  and  every  tenet  in  religion.
Still,  he  could  not  but  feel  the  mighty  impulse
which  the  mind  of  all  Europe  had  received  from
the  Reformation,  from  great  discoveries  and  in-
ventions, and  from  the  revival  of  physical  as  well
as  classical  learning.  These  causes,  conspiring
with  the  native  ardour  and  boldness  of  his  own
genius,  could  not  fail  to  make  him  a  reformer  in
the  world  of  thought ;  while  the  circumstances  al-
ready referred  to  will  explain  why  his  reforms
were  applied  to  science  rather  than  to  practical  life,

XVI  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

and  why  to  physical  rather  than  to  moral  or  po-
litical  science.*

It  was  Locke's  fortune,  on  the  other  hand,  to  live
when  the  emancipation  of  thought,  which  had  been
commenced,  and  in  one  sense  secured  by  the  Ref-
ormation, was  more  complete ;  when,  after  a  long
struggle,  England  had  gained  political  and  religious
liberty,  and  the  human  mind  was  consequently  en-
couraged to  enlarge  to  the  utmost  the  sphere  of  its
activities.  In  the  department  of  physical  science,
great  advances  had  already  been  made.  Within
the  brief  space  of  fifty  years,  under  the  guidance
of  Bacon,  the  labours  of  Galileo,  Kepler,  Newton,
Des  Cartes,  Boyle,  and  Harvey  had  changed  the
whole  face  of  nature  to  the  eye  of  the  scientific
inquirer,  and  had  placed  mankind  on  a  vantage
ground,  of  which  even  fancy  had  hardly  ventured
to  dream.  It  was  natural,  then,  that  an  original
thinker  like  Locke,  animated  by  the  love  of  liberty
and  pledged  to  no  system,  should  apply  himself  to
a  corresponding  improvement  in  mental  philoso-
phy. As  Bacon  had  been  led,  both  by  the  imme-
diate necessities  of  science  and  by  the  state  of  Pol-

itics,  to  draw  his  illustrations  almost  entirely  fronri
nature,  similar  causes  led  Locke  to  derive  his,
chiefly,  from  man  and  from  society.  The  method
of  investigation  laid  down  by  Bacon,  though  in-
tended  to  be  coextensive  with  the  whole  field  of
human  inquiry,*  was  yet,  as  he  left  it,  more  imme-
diately applicable  to  physics.  It  was  the  part  of
Locke  to  show  by  example,  even  more  than  by
precept,  how  the  same  method  might  be  extended
to  Metaphysics,  and  to  attempt  for  it  what  had
already  been  attempted  and  achieved  in  Astron-
omy  and  Mechanics.  By  one,  the  true  method
of  philosophizing  was  deduced  from  a  general  sur-
vey of  the  nature  of  knowledge,  and  of  the  rela-
tion between  the  human  mind  and  the  universe ;
by  the  other,  it  was  derived  from  a  more  exact
analysis  of  the  mind  itself,  especially  when  em-
ployed  in  objective  investigation.     Bacon  had  con-

XVlll  BACON    AND   LOCKE.

stantly  in  his  eye  the  errors  and  absurdities  of  the
alchymists  on  one  hand,  and  of  the  disciples  of
Aristotle  on  the  other.  Locke  had  been  called  by
the  circumstances  of  his  life  to  observe  more  care-
fully the  fallacies  prevalent  among  divines  and  men
of  the  world.  Both  alike  founded  all  acquired
knowledge  on  experience — but  with  one  it  was
an  experience  informed  and  directed  by  system —
from  which  particular  facts  ascended  step  by  step  to
general  laws,  and  which,  aided  as  it  was  in  physical
researches  by  the  most  exact  tests,  succeeded  in
investing  its  conclusions  with  a  high  degree  of  pre-
cision and  certainty.  With  the  other  it  was  an
experience  less  rigorous  and  formal,  which  aspired
rather  after  maxims  of  practical  wisdom  than  after
precise  inductions,  and  which,  though  useful  in
every  species  of  inquiry,  would  still  prove  most
valuable  when  employed  in  politics  or  in  religion.
The  precise  services  rendered  by  each  of  these
great  men  to  the  cause  of  sound  philosophy  are
often  misapprehended.  Bacon,  for  instance,  is  re-
garded by  many  as  the  first  who  taught  that  science
must  be  derived  from  experiment  and  observation.
This  is  a  mistake.  To  say  nothing  of  Aristotle
himself,  who  exemplifies  in  his  own  practice  a
strong  sense  of  the  value  of  observed  facts  as
materials  for  science,  the  Middle  Ages  certainly
gave  birth  to  a  school  founded  on  experiment.     No

BA.CON    AND    LOCKE.  XIX

men  ever  evinced  a  greater  contempt  for  mere  au*
thority,  or  for  the  old  legislators  of  science,  nor
did  any  ever  profess  a  greater  desire  to  consult  ex-
perience, than  Paracelsus,  Van  Helmont,  and  the
other  early  experimenters  in  chymistry.  Their
great  error  was,  that  their  experiments  and  obser-
vations were  made  without  system.  Their  facts
were  neither  observed  with  accuracy,  nor  arranged
with  skill,  nor  properly  compared  and  confronted
with  each  other,  nor  sufficiently  multiplied.  Hence,
with  a  few  precious  results,  their  labours  brought
forth  an  abundant  brood  of  extravagant  hopes  and
monstrous  reveries.  All  this  was  clearly  seen  by
Bacon.  He  plainly  saw  (and  it  constitutes  one  of
his  chief  merits)  that,  in  adopting  the  empirical
method  of  investigation,  as  it  was  then  termed
by  way  of  distinction,  these  men  had  renounced
reason,  and  had  placed  themselves  under  the  gui-
dance of  mere  fancy.  In  his  noble  preface  to  the
Instauratio  Magna,  published  in  1620,  he  thus  re-
fers to  this  class  of  inquirers  :*  "If  any  inconcern-
ed  natures,  not  mancipate  to  others'  or  their  own
opinions,  but  affecting  liberty,  have  been  so  far  an-
imated as  to  desire  that  others  with  themselves
would   make  farther   inquiry,  these   surely  have

*  Not  having  at  hand  the  original  of  this  preface  in  Latin,  we
use  the  rather  indifferent  translatien  of  Gilbert  Watts,  publish-
ed 1640.

meant  well,  but  performed  little ;  for  they  seem  to
have  proceeded  on  probable  grounds  only,  being
wheeled  about  in  a  vertigenous  maze  of  arguments,
and  by  a  promiscuous  license  of  inquiry  have  in-
deed loosened  the  sinews  of  severe  inquisition  ;  nor
hath  any  of  all  these  with  a  just  patience  and  suffi-
cient expectance  attended  the  operations  of  nature
and  the  successes  of  experience. ^^  He  adds  after-
ward, "  It  is  an  error  of  special  note,  that  the  indus-
try bestowed  in  experiments  hath  presently,  upon
the  first  access  into  the  business,  by  a  too  forward
and  unseasonable  desire,  seized  upon  some  designed
operation  ;  I  mean,  sought  after  Fructifera  non  Lu-
cifera,  Experiments  of  Use,  and  not  Experiments
of  Light  and  Discovery ;  not  imitating  the  divine
method,  which  created  the  first  day  Light  only,  and
allowed  it  one  entire  day,  producing  no  material
work  the  same  day,  but  descended  to  their  creation
the  days  following."  He  then  states,  that  in  mat-
ters of  such  perplexed  difficulty  there  is  no  relying
upon  the  judgment  of  men  from  their  own  abilities,
or  upon  the  casual  felicity  of  particular  events ;
for  neither  the  capacity  of  man,  how  excellent  so-
ever, nor  the  chance  of  experience,  never  so  often
iterated  and  essayed,  is  of  force  to  conquer  these
mysteries.  We  must  march  by  line  and  level,  and
all  the  way,  even  from  the  first  perception  of  senses,
must  be  secured  and  fortified  by  a  certain  rule  aad

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  XXI

constant  method  of  proceeding;"  and  thus  he  pro-
poses  to  "  estahlish  a  true  and  legitimate  marriage
between  the  empirical  and  rational  faculty,  whose
fastidious  and  unfortunate  divorce  and  separation
hath  troubled  and  disordered  the  whole  race  and
^generation  of  mankind."

For  this  great  work  Bacon  was  eminently  qual-
ified. His  commanding  genius,  enriched  by  an-
cient  learning,  and  disciplined  and  invigorated  by
the  conflicts  of  the  court  and  the  forum,*  enabled
him  to  rise  to  that  point  from  which  he  could  sur-
vey the  whole  field  of  human  inquiry,  and  yet  not
lose  sight,  through  a  too  excursive  fancy,  of  each
separate  part  of  it.  To  him,  too,  was  vouchsafed
a  clear  perception  both  of  the  true  ends  of  knowl-
edge and  of  the  spirit  in  which  it  becomes  us  to
pursue  it.  "  In  sum,"  says  he,  "  I  would  advise  all
in  general  that  they  take  into  serious  consideration
the  true  and  genuine  ends  of  knowledge  ;  that  they
seek  it  not  either  for  pleasure,  or  contention,  or

contempt  of  others,  or  for  profit,  or  fame,  or  for
honour  and  promotion,  or  such  like  adulterate  and

and  desert  paths  ;  and  by  the  protection  and  assist,

and  prepared  armies,  as  it  were,  of  opinions,  and

nature,  and  everywhere  flying  fancies  ;  that  so  we

safe  and  sound  indications  and  impressions  of  truth.

profciencie,  surely  by  no  other  means  have  we
cleared  ourselves  a  way  than  by  a  sincere  and  just

erations o^  naiure.^^  "And  seeing  these  perform-

not  within  tiie  compass  of  our  meer  natural  pow.
er  and  command,  we  do  here,  in  the  access  to
this  work,  pour  forth  humblest  and  most  ardent
supplications  to  God  the  Father,  God  the  Word,

of  mankind,  and  of  the  pilgrimage  of  this  life,
wherein  we  wear  out  few  and  evil  days,  they  would
vouchsafe  to  endow  mankind,  by  my  hand,  with  new
Donatives.  And,  moreover,  we  humbly  pray  that
Humane  Knowledges  may  no  way  impeach  or  prej-
udice Divine  Truths  ;  nor  that, from  the  disclosing
of  the  ways  of  sense,  and  the  letting  in  of  a  more
plentiful  natural  light,  any  mists  of  incredulity  or
clouds  of  darkness  arise  in  our  minds  touching  Di.
vine  Mysteries;  hut  rather  from  a  purified  intellect,
purged  from  fancies  and  vanity ;  and  yet,  yielded
and  absolutely  rendered  up  to  Divine  Oracles,  the
tributes  of  faith  may  be  rendered  to  faith.  In  the
last  place,  that  the  venome  of  knowledge  infused
by  the  serpent,  whereby  the  mind  of  man  is  swell-
ed and  blown  up,  being  voided,  we  may  not  be  too
aspiringly  wise  or  above  sobriety,  but  may  improve
and  propagate  verity  by  charity."*

The  reader  can  hardly  need  to  be  reminded  that
Bacon  himself  did  not  aspire  to  be  a  discoverer  in
any  particular  branch  of  knowledge.     He  was  con-
tented (to  borrow  his  own  quaint  but  significant
*  Introduction  to  Inst.  Mag.

XXIV      .  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

language  from  a  letter  to  Cecil)  "  to  awake  better
spirits,  being  hinaself  like  a  bell-ringer,  who  is  the
first  to  call  others  to  church."  He  was  not  even
remarkably  conversant  with  the  existing  state  of
science.  But  his  great  sagacity  and  vigorous  sense
enabled  him  to  perceive  that,  while  all  these  sci-
ences had  been  abundantly  fruitful  of  disputations
and  contentions,  they  had  hitherto  done  little  "  for
the  life  of  man."  He  knew  well  what  it  was  that
knowledge  ought  to  do  for  man — even  "  the  resti-
tution of  his  sovereignty  over  nature."  To  a  deep
conviction  that  in  this  end  it  had  signally  failed,  he
united  the  penetration  which  could  discover  at  once
the  causes  of  failure  and  the  means  of  success.  To
draw  from  this  failure  the  means  of  a  future  and  un-
limited progress  in  knowledge  and  happiness;  to
make  men  distrustful  of  all  learning  which  does  not
contribute  to  their  physical  or  social  welfare,  or  to
their  increased  refinement  and  dignity  ;  to  animate
them  with  the  noble  desire  of  "  enlarging  the  bounds
of  human  empire  to  the  effecting  all  things  possi-
ble ;"  and  to  inculcate  the  great  and  much-neglect-
ed  lesson  that  the  enlargement  of  knowledge  should
never  be  separated  from  the  growth  of  virtue,  but
that,  properly  understood,  "  truth  and  goodness
were  one,  differing  but  as  the  seal  and  the  print —
for  truth  prints  goodness :"  these  were  the  high
aims  of  Bacon.    Cowley,  in  his  fine  and  well-known

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  XXV

Ode  to  the  Royal  Society,  has  pronounced  his  true
eulogium.

"  Bacon,  like  Moses,  led  us  forth  at  last,
The  barren  wilderness  he  pass'd,
Did  on  the  very  border  stand
Of  the  bless'd  promised  land',
And  from  the  mountain  top  of  his  exalted  wit,
Saw  it  himself,  and  showed  us  it."

In  view  of  such  services,  animated  by  a  spirit  at
once  so  humble  and  yet  so  hopeful,  and  after  the
lapse  of  two  centuries,  during  which,  under  the  con-
duct of  this  Prophet  of  Arts,  mankind  have  done
more  to  gain  a  dominion  over  nature,  and  to  rise
to  an  adequate  and  devout  understanding  of  its  mys-
teries, than  had  been  done  during  the  five  thousand
years  which  preceded ;  at  such  a  time  it  cannot
but  excite  a  feeling  of  surprise,  not  unmingled  with
scorn,  to  find  men — and  those,  too,  in  some  cases,
who  have  never  taken  the  trouble  to  read  him —
speak  contemptuously  of  Bacon,  reverting,  with
many  professions  of  regret,  to  the  reign  of  the  Sta-
gyrite,  or  the  golden  visions  of  Plato.  It  could
move  no  special  regret  in  others  if  such  minds
were  privileged  to  surround  themselves  once  more
with  the  glimmer  of  the  schools ;  but  it  will  prob-
ably remain  a  subject  of  gratitude  to  most  men  not
bent  on  rejecting  the  gifts  of  Providence,  that  they
have  lived  after  the  time  of  Bacon,  and  are  permit-
C

XXVI  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

ted  to  sit  in  the  light  of  truths  which  have  been  re
vealed  by  his  followers.

In  assigning  to  Locke  his  proper  place  among
philosophers,  some  embarrassment  is  felt  at  a  time
when  opinions  so  conflicting  prevail  in  regard  to
him.     On  the  one  hand,  it  is  obvious  that  he  was
less  qualified  than  Bacon  by  the  nature  of  his  ge-
nius to  point  out  to  mankind  the  true  way  to  dis-
covery ;  yet,  on  the  other,  it  must  be  conceded  that
he  made  more  positive  contributions  to  the  sum  of
human  knowledge.     If  Bacon  awakened  a  new
spirit  of  inquiry,  Locke  had  the  wisdom  to  chasten
and  regulate  it.     The  one  laid  down  rules  by  which
science  was  to  be  advanced ;  the  other  cultivated
among  thinking  men  the  intellectual  habits  and  dis-
positions, without  which  such  rules  would  have  been
of  little  avail.     If  the  one  marshalled  and  led  on
the  hosts  of  reform,  the  other  gave  the  prudent
counsels  and  presented  the  instructive  example  by
which  victory  was  secured.     At  a  time  when  it
•has  become  fashionable  to  depreciate  the  theories
of  this  great  writer,  it  may  be  well  to  remember
that  one  may  construct  erroneous  systems,  and  yet
in  the  vory  act  exemplify  some  of  the  noblest  traits,
and  render  some  of  the  most  signal  services,  which
belong  to  true  philosophy.     If  he  exhibits  himself,
and  excites  in  others  a  fearless  spirit  of  inquiry,
combined  with  a  modesty  and  prudence  which  ren-

BACON    AND    LOCKE.  XXVU

der  that  spirit  harmless ;  if  he  cultivates  a  taste
for  clear  and  precise  thinking ;  weakens  the  do-
minion of  prejudice  ;  charms  away  the  rage  for
mere  verbal  disputes ;  and  gives  new  impulse  to
our  love  of  truth,  as  well  as  to  our  charity  for  error,
he  may  more  than  atone  for  many  defects.  Such
we  believe  to  have  been  the  case  with  John  Locke.
In  common  with  many  others  whom  he  has  taught
to  detect  his  own  faults,  we  cannot  but  deplore  his
want  of  a  more  spiritual  philosophy.  We  admit
that  his  ethical  views  are  too  sordid  ;  that  his  ac-
count of  the  origin  of  our  knowledge  is  incomplete  ;
that  some  of  his  political  and  religious  doctrines
are  liable  to  misapplication ;  and  that  his  exposi-
tion of  principles  is  sometimes  vague  and  unsatis-
factory. Yet  we  may  still  acknowledge  our  pro-
found admiration  and  respect  for  a  writer  whom
we  can  never  dip  into  without  improvement,  nor
without  sympathizing  in  the  following  commenda-
tion by  another :  "  There  is  scarcely  an  event  of
our  lives,"  says  this  writer,  "  to  which  we  look  back
with  more  lively  recollection  than  to  the  period
when  we  first  read  the  Essay  upon  the  Human  Un-
derstanding.  It  still  remains  in  our  memory,  like
an  era  in  the  history  of  our  thoughts,  from  which
we  seem  to  date  a  sort  of  revolution  in  the  very
constitution  of  our  knowledge.  For  it  is  not  with
a  view  to  opinions  that  the  writings  of  Locke  are

XXVIU        BACON  AND  LOCKE.

to  be  studied,  but  rather  for  the  sake  of  witnessing
the  operation  of  his  mind.  There  runs  through
his  Essay  such  a  vein  of  precise  and  admirable  re-
flection ;  he  places  his  thoughts,  right  or  wrong,
in  so  clear  a  light ;  distinguishes  and  discards  all
trifling  and  merely  verbal  disputes  ;  makes  us  un-
derstand  ourselves  so  unequivocally  in  the  words
which  we  employ,  and  in  the  subject  upon  which
we  are  meditating,  that  we  know  not  any  work
that  could  be  named  in  which  the  exercise  of  think-
ing may  be  so  safely  taken.  This  is  never  so
strongly  felt  as  when  we  come  to  his  writings  fresh
from  the  pages  of  some  modern  metaphysidan.  It
is  like  changing  the  smoky  atmosphere  of  a  city
for  some  pure  and  mountain  air  ;  the  mind  feels  as
if  it  were  inhaling  health  from  the  very  thoughts
which  it  breathes  ;  so  much  singleness,  and  direct-
ness, and  integrity  is  there  about  all  his  opinions ;
such  a  contempt  for  paradox ;  such  superiority  to
all  the  little  tricks  by  which  the  commonplace
thoughts  of  commonplace  minds  are  trimmed  out
in  the  present  day,  and  decked,  if  we  may  so  ex-
press ourselves,  in  the  mere  cast-off*  clothes  of  real
learning  and  philosophy."*

While  I  should  be  disposed  slightly  to  qualify
the   praise  which  is  here  awarded  to  Locke  for
clearness  and  precision,  I  should  claim  more  for
*  Qu.  Review,  vol.  xxvi.

BACON   AND    LOCKE.  XXIX

him  as  a  discoverer.  In  the  estimation  of  Sir
James  Mackintosh,  than  whom  it  would  be  difficult
to  find  a  critic  more  able  or  unbiased,  his  Essay-
still  stands  the  most  conspicuous  landmark  in  the
progress  of  metaphysical  philosophy  for  the  last
two  centuries.  He  adds,  ^Hf  Locke  made  few  dis-
coveries, Socrates  made  none.  Yet  both  did  more
for  the  improvement  of  the  understanding,  and  not
less  for  the  progress  of  knowledge,  than  the  au-
thors of  the  most  brilliant  discoveries,^*  And  Mr.
Stewart,  by  no  means  a  partial  critic,  has  pro-
nounced the  first  two  books  on  the  Human  Under-
standing, which  to  many  appear  hardly  equal  to
the  third  and  fourth,  as  "a  precious  accession  to
the  theory  of  the  human  mind ;  as  the  richest
contribution  of  well-observed  and  well-described
facts  which  was  ever  bequeathed  by  a  single  indi-
vidual ;  and  as  the  indisputable,  though  not  always
acknowledged,  source  of  some  of  the  most  refined
conclusions  with  respect  to  the  intellectual  phe-
nomena  which  have  since  been  brought  to  light
by  succeeding  inquirers."*

It  has  been  frequently  alleged  that  neither  Ba-
con nor  Locke  were  appreciated  by  their  contem-
poraries, and  that  their  works  had  to  win  their
way  to  favour  by  slow  degrees,  even  among  their
own  countrymen.     In   the  case  of  Bacon   there

*  Prelim.  Diss.,  Part  II.
C2

XXX  BACON    AND    LOCKE.

seems  to  have  been  foundation  for  this  remark
only  in  England.  That  touching  passage  in  his
will,  in  which  he  bequeaths  "  his  name  and  fame  to
foreign  nations,  and  to  his  own  after  some  time  he
passed,''^  shows  how  well  he  foresaw  the  effect
which  the  remembrance  of  his  official  life  was  to
have  on  the  success  of  his  philosophy  at  home.
The  other  nations  to  which  he  bequeathed  his
fame  were  faithful  to  the  trust.  Osborne  tells  us
that,  "  but  for  an  invincible  strength  of  contrary
judgments  that  came  to  his  rescue  from  beyond
the  sea,  his  own  countrymen  would  have  cashiered
his  Advancement  of  Learning  as  an  heretical  and
impertinent  piece  ;"  and  he  adds,  that  "  the  censure
which  fell  upon  Raleigh  as  an  atheist,  because  he
sailed  aloof  from  the  beaten  track  of  the  schools,
which  Queen  Elizabeth  regarded  as  against  God
and  her  father's  honour,  whose  faith  (if  he  owned
any)  was  grounded  on  school  divinity  :"  "  a  like
censure,"  he  says,  "fell  upon  venerable  Bacon,  till
overhalanced  ly  a  greater  glory  from  strangers."
It  is  evident,  from  a  passage  quoted  by  Sir  James
Mackintosh  (from  the  life  of  Des  Cartes,  by  Baillet),
that  when  the  news  of  Bacon's  death  reached
Paris,  it  sensibly  affected  all  those  who  aspired  to
the  establishment  of  a  new  philosophy,  and  who
knew  that  Bacon  had  been  engaged  in  that  design
for  several  years.     Even  during  his  life  he  re-

BACON   AND   LOCKE.  XXXI

ceived  from  such  men  as  Gassendi  and  Richelieu
the  most  marked,  and,  in  some  instances,  enthusias-
tic expressions  of  regard.  Individuals  went  from
France  "  across  the  seas  for  no  other  reason  but
to  see  him,  and  gain  an  opportunity  of  discoursing
with  him."  It  was  to  one  of  these,  who  paid  him
the  fulsome  compliment  of  comparing  him  to  an-
gels, "  of  whom  he  had  heard  much,  but  had  not
seen,"  that  he  made  the  well-known  reply,  "  If  the
politeness  of  others  compare  me  to  an  angel,  my
own  infirmities  remind  me  that  I  am  a  man."
Editions  of  his  larger  works  appeared  in  some  in-
stances in  France  immediately  after  they  were  first
published,  and  were  repeated  both  there  and  in
Holland  several  times  within  a  few  years,  whereas
in  his  own  country  the  Novum  Organum  was  never
published  separately,  and  no  complete  edition  of
all  his  works  appeared  till  more  than  a  century
after  his  death.  The  date  of  his  fame  in  England
is  fixed  by  Stewart  at  the  time  of  the  institution
of  the  Royal  Society.

The  success  of  Locke  with  his  own  country-
men was  more  immediate.  Warburton,  in  one  of
his  letters,*  says, "There  is  something  very  different
in  the  fortune  of  Malebranche  and  Locke.  When
Malebranche  first  appeared,  it  was  with  a  general
applause  and  admiration !  when  Locke  first  pub-

*  Letter  CXXVII.

XXXll  BACON    AND   LOCKE.

lished  his  Essay,  he  had  hardly  a  single  approver.
Now  Locke  is  universal,  and  Malebranche  sunk
into  obscurity."  And  again,  in  the  same  letter,
"  The  sage  Locke  supported  himself  by  no  system
on  the  one  hand,  nor  on  the  other  did  he  dishon-
our himself  by  any  whimsies  ;  the  consequence  of
which  was,  that  neither  following  the  fashion  nor
striking  the  imagination,  he  at  first  had  neither
followers  nor  admirers."  These  statements  can
hardly  be  reconciled  with  the  well-known  fact
that  his  great  work,  the  Essay,  passed  through
several  editions  soon  after  its  first  appearance ;  a
sixth,  if  not  a  later,  having  received  the  author's
own  corrections.*  Indeed,  the  connexion  of  Locke
with  the  popular  party  of  the  day,  the  services
which  he  had  rendered  to  it  by  his  pen,  as  well  as
his  sufferings  in  its  behalf,  must  have  all  contribu-
ted  to  secure  readers  for  any  work  bearing  his
name,  though  the  age  was  by  no  means  partial  to
metaphysical  speculations.  The  obloquy  to  which
the  Essay  was  exposed,  on  account  of  the  sup-
posed tendency  of  some  of  its  doctrines,  and  the
controversies  into  which  its  author  was  drawn  with
Bishop  Stillingfleet  and  others,  only  helped  its
success ;  since  in  these  controversies  Locke  was
thought,  at   the  time — how  justly  we   need   not

*  See  Preface  in  the  American  edition  published  in  1818  by
Collins  &  Hannay.

BACON    AND   LOCKE.  XXXIU

now  inquire — to  have  been  completely  triumphant.
There  can  be  no  doubt  that  from  these  causes,  as
well  as  from  its  great  intrinsic  merit,  this  work  re-
ceived an  earlier  and  more  cordial  reception  than
was  often,  if  ever,  accorded  in  England  to  any  sim-
ilar treatise ;  and,  as  Hallam  has  remarked,  consid.
erably  more  than  half  a  century  was  afterward  to
elapse  before  any  writer  in  our  language  (nor  was
the  case  very  different  in  France,  after  the  patron-
age accorded  to  it  by  Voltaire)  could,  with  much
chance  of  success,  question  any  leadinsj  doctrine
of  its  author.

The  tract  in  this  volume  on  the  Conduct  of  the
Undersianding  was  not  published  till  after  the  au-
thor's  death,  and  is  free  from  the  faults  which  have
been  justly  imputed  to  some  portions  of  the  Essay.
It  seems  to  have  been  originally  intended  as  a  sup-
plement to  that  great  work,  and  points  out  the  du-
ties which  devolve  upon  us  in  consequence  of  our
possessing  intellectual  faculties  that  are  subject  to
error  and  perversion.  He  insists  especially  on
the  sophisms  by  which  we  are  liable  to  impose  upon
ourselves,  and  warns  us  against  the  insidious  influ-
ence of  prejudice,  partiality,  self-interest,  vanity,
and  inattention.

The  Essays  of  Bacon  were  first  published  in
1595,  more  than  30  years  before  his  death,  and

XXXIV        BACON  AND  LOCKE.

were  at  that  time  but  ten  in  number.*  They  sub-
sequently passed  through  several  editions,  and
were  gradually  enlarged  and  amended  until  they
reached  their  present  form,  in  which  they  were  first
printed  about  a  year  before  the  close  of  the  au-
thor's life.  Several  of  them  seem  to  have  been  in-
tended as  a  popular  sketch  of  views  and  opinions
which  were  to  be  put  forth  more  formally  in  his
later  and  larger  works,  especially  in  his  Advance-
ment of  Learning,  and  in  his  treatise  De  Augmentis
ScienUarunif  which  is  but  an  enlargement  of  the
same  work.  In  some  instances  the  Essays  will  be
found  identical  both  in  sentiment  and  language  with
portions  of  these  works. f  They  afford  through-
out impressive  illustrations  of  the  justice  of  the  fol-
lowing discriminating  remarks  of  Mr.  Hallam,  with
which  I  shall  close  this  Introduction  :  "  He  (Bacon)
was  more  eminently  the  philosopher  of  human  than
of  general  nature.  Hence  he  is  exact  as  well  as
profound  in  all  his  reflections  on  civil  life  and  man-
kind, while  his  conjectures  in  natural  philosophy,
though  often  very  acute,  are  apt  to  wander  far  from

the  truth,  in  consequence  of  his  defective  acquaint-

THE     LORD     CHANCELLOR     BACON,

PREFATORY   EPISTLES.

To  Mr.  Anthony  Bacon,  his  dear  Brother.

Loving  and  beloved  brother,  I  do  now  like  some
that  have  an  orchard  ill-neighboured,  that  gather  their
fruit  before  it  is  ripe,  to  prevent  stealing.  These
fragments  of  my  conceits  were  going  to  print :  to  la-
bour the  stay  of  them  had  been  troublesome,  and
subject  to  interpretation  ;  to  let  them  pass  had  been
to  adventure  the  wrong  they  might  receive  by  untrue
copies,  or  by  some  garnishment  which  it  might  please
any  that  should  set  them  forth  to  bestow  upon  them ;
therefore  I  held  it  best  discretion  to  publish  them
myself,  as  they  passed  long  ago  from  my  pen,  with-
out any  farther  disgrace  than  the  weakness  of  the
author;  and  as  I  did  ever  hold  there  might  be  as
great  a  vanity  in  retiring  and  withdrawing  men's  con-
ceits (except  they  be  of  some  nature)  from  the  world
as  in  obtruding  them,  so  in  these  particulars  I  have
played  myself  the  inquisitor,  and  find  nothing  to  my
understanding  in  them  contrary  or  infectious  to  the
state  of  religion  or  manners,  but  rather,  as  I  suppose,
medicinable  :  only  1  disliked  now  to  put  them  out,  be-
cause they  will  be  like  the  late  new  halfpence,  which,
though  the  silver  were  good,  yet  the  pieces  were
small ;  but  since  they  would  not  stay  with  their  mas-
ter, but  would  needs  travel  abroad,  I  have  preferred
them  to  you  that  are  next  myself;  dedicating  them,
such  as  they  are,  to  our  love,  in  the  depth  whereof,
lassure  you,  so  etimes  I  wish  your  infirmities  trans-
lated upon  myself,  that  her  majesty  might  have  the
service  of  so  active  and  able  a  mind  ;  and  I  might  be

40  PREFATORY    EPISTLES.

with  excuse  confined  to  these  contemplations  and
studies,  for  which  I  am  fittest :  so  commend  I  you  to
the  preservation  of  the  Divine  Majesty.
Your  entire  loving  brother,

Fran.  Bacon.
From  my  chamber  at  Gray's  Inn,  )
this  30th  of  January,  1597.         J

To  my  loving  Brother^  Sir  John  Constable,  Kt.

My  last  Essays  I  dedicated  to  my  dear  brother,  Mr.
Anthony  Bacon,  who  is  with  God.  Looking  among
my  papers  this  vacation,  I  found  others  of  the  same
nature,  which,  if  I  myself  shall  not  suffer  to  be  lost,  it
seemeth  the  world  will  not,  by  the  often  printing  of
the  former.  Missing  my  brother,  I  found  you  next ;
in  respect  of  bond,  both  of  near  alliance,  and  of  straight
friendship  and  society,  and  particularly  of  communi-
cation in  studies,  wherein  I  must  acknowledge  my-
self beholden  to  you ;  for  as  my  business  found  rest
in  my  contemplations,  so  my  contemplations  ever
found  rest  in  your  loving  conference  and  judgment :
so  wishing  you  all  good,  I  remain

Your  loving  brother  and  friend,

1612.  Fra.  Bacon.

To  the  Right  Honourable  my  very  good  lord  the  BuJce
of  Buckingham^  his  grace  Lord  High  Admiral  of  Eng-
land.

Excellent  Lord,
Solomon  says  "A  good  name  is  as  a  precious  oint-
ment;" and  I  assure  myself  such  will  your  grace's
name  be  with  posterity ;  for  your  fortune  and  merit
both  have  be-en  eminent,  and  you  have  planted  things
that  are  like  to  last.  I  do  now  publish  my  Essays,
which,  of  all  my  other  works,  have  been  most  cur-

rent ;  for  that,  as  it  seems,  they  come  home  to  men's
business  and  bosoms.  I  have  enlarged  them  both  in
number  and  weight;  so  that  they  are  indeed  a  new
work :  I  thought  it  therefore  agreeable  to  my  affec-

before  them,  both  in  English  and  Latin ;  for  I  do  con-
ceive that  the  Latin  volume  of  them,  being  in  the  uni-
versal language,  may  last  as  long  as  books  last.  My
Instauration  1  dedicated  to  the  king ;  my  History  of
Henry  the  Seventh,  which  1  have  now  translated
into  Latin,  and  my  portions  of  Natural  History,  to
the  Prince  ;  and  these  I  dedicate  to  your  grace,  being

God  gives  to  my  pen  and  labours,  I  could  yield.  God
lead  your  grace  by  the  hand.

Your  grace's  most  obliged

And  faithful  servant,

Fr.  St.  Albans.
